Now we need a rigorous proof.

Given the difficulty, maybe we should attempt an analytic proof with coordinates, but try to simplify using algebraic manipulations. Since we have parameters m,n,h, and r,R defined in terms of them, we can compute everything in terms of m,n,h. The expressions might simplify nicely if we use the relationships.

Let’s set:

- Let M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with 0 < m < n.
- Let A = (0,h) with h>0.
- Then r = MA = √(m^2+h^2), R = NA = √(n^2+h^2).
- C = (m - r, 0) because on Ω on the ray from M opposite N. So C_x = m - r.
- D = (n + R, 0) because on Γ on the ray from N opposite M. So D_x = n + R.
- P is circumcenter of ACD. Since C and D have y=0, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through midpoint: x = ( (m - r) + (n + R) )/2 = (m + n - r + R)/2. So p_x = (m + n - r + R)/2.
- p_y is found from perpendicular bisector of AC. A=(0,h), C=(m - r,0). Midpoint AC: ((m - r)/2, h/2). Slope AC: (0 - h)/( (m - r) - 0 ) = -h/(m - r) = h/(r - m) (since m - r negative, r - m positive). So perpendicular slope = -(r - m)/h. So line: y - h/2 = -(r - m)/h (x - (m - r)/2). At x = p_x, we get y = h/2 - (r - m)/h (p_x - (m - r)/2). Compute p_x - (m - r)/2 = (m+n - r + R)/2 - (m - r)/2 = (n + R)/2. So p_y = h/2 - (r - m)(n+R)/(2h). So p_y = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R))/(2h). This is correct.

Now define v = P - A = (p_x, p_y - h). Compute p_y - h = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R))/(2h) - h = (h^2 - (r - m)(n+R) - 2h^2)/(2h) = -(h^2 + (r - m)(n+R))/(2h). So v = (p_x, - (h^2 + (r - m)(n+R))/(2h) ). Let’s denote T = h^2 + (r - m)(n+R). Then v = (p_x, -T/(2h)). So v_y negative.

Now we need E and F. As derived, t_E = (2m p_x + T)/|v|^2, t_F = (2n p_x + T)/|v|^2, where |v|^2 = p_x^2 + T^2/(4h^2). But we might not need explicit t_E, t_F; we can compute E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. So E = (t_E p_x, h - (t_E T)/(2h)), F = (t_F p_x, h - (t_F T)/(2h)). Since t_E, t_F are scalars. Also note that B = (0,-h).

Now we want circle through B, E, F. Let's denote coordinates:

B = (0, -h)
E = (x_E, y_E) with x_E = t_E p_x, y_E = h - (t_E T)/(2h)
F = (x_F, y_F) with x_F = t_F p_x, y_F = h - (t_F T)/(2h)

We also have that t_E and t_F satisfy: from earlier, t_E = (2m p_x + T)/|v|^2, t_F = (2n p_x + T)/|v|^2. So these are known.

Now we need to show that line L: through H parallel to v (direction v) is tangent to circle (BEF). H = (p_x, (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/p_y). Note p_y is negative.

We can try to compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then compute the condition that the distance from its center to line L equals its radius. But maybe we can find a more elegant condition: For a given line L through H, L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but that's not directly.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that if we take a line through H with direction v, it is tangent if and only if the line is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency. Actually, if we param: (x,y) = A + t v. Then |A + t v|^2 = r^2 gives t=0 and t = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2. So E = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Similarly, F = A - 2 ((A-c)·v)/|v|^2 v.

Now B = (a, -h) (conjugate of A). So we have three points B, E, F.

We want to show that line L through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M=(0,0), N=(c,0), P=(p, y_P). As derived, H = (p, p(c-p)/y_P). Since y_P is negative, H is below x-axis.

Now we need to prove that the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to line L equals the radius of (BEF). That is a condition that can be expressed as: the line L is tangent to circle (BEF). Equivalent to: the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but that's not helpful.

Another way: Show that the line L is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF) and that A lies on the circle? No.

Perhaps we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) in terms of A, B, and the line AP. Since E and F are on line AP, we can find the equation of the circle through B and the two points on line AP. Let the line AP have equation: l: y = α x + β (with α = (y_P-h)/(p-a)? Actually we can find α,β from A and P. But maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter from the midpoint of BH to something? Or that the center O is the midpoint of the segment joining the midpoints of BH and something? In numeric, O = (4.51,-9.605). H=(4.915,-0.255). B=(0,-3). Midpoint of BH = (2.4575,-3.1275). That's far.

Maybe we can find that O is the reflection of P across the midpoint of something? Not.

Given the difficulty, perhaps we should try to use vector geometry with coordinates and simplify the algebra using the known relations. Let's attempt to compute the circle (BEF) directly using coordinates and then compute the distance from its center to line L. We can try to derive a condition that the line L is tangent if and only if some expression is zero, and then verify that expression holds identically using the relations. This will be a brute-force algebraic verification. Since the problem is from an olympiad, the solution likely uses synthetic reasoning, but we can produce an analytic proof that is rigorous and maybe simpler. However, we need to present it clearly.

Let's set up coordinates with M=(0,0), N=(c,0), A=(a,h) with h>0, and the relations:

(1) a^2 + h^2 = r^2.
(2) (a-c)^2 + h^2 = R^2.
We also have r < R, so c > 0? Actually from (2) we have R^2 = (a-c)^2+h^2 = a^2-2ac+c^2+h^2 = r^2 -2ac+c^2 => 2ac = r^2 - R^2 + c^2 => a = (c^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2c). So a is determined by c,r,R. And h^2 = r^2 - a^2. Since r<R, a may be positive or negative? In our numeric, a=-4, so negative. So a could be negative. But we can keep as is.

Now we have C = (-r,0), D = (c+R,0). Then P = (p, y_P) with p = (c+R-r)/2, y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). So y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). Also, note that (c+R-r) = 2p.

Now we need to compute E and F. Let v = (v_x, v_y) = P - A = (p - a, y_P - h). Compute v_x = p - a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. v_y = y_P - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h. We'll keep as v.

Now compute A·v = a v_x + h v_y. But we might not need that explicitly. Instead, we can find E and F using the chord property: For line through A, the other intersection with circle centered at origin is given by: E = (r^2 / |A|^2) * A? That's for a circle centered at origin and a line through A? Actually, if we have a circle centered at origin, and a line through point A on the circle, the second intersection E is given by: E = (r^2 / |A|^2) * A? That would be A itself because |A|=r, so (r^2/|A|^2)=1, so E=A. That's not right. Wait, there is a formula: For a circle centered at origin, if a line through A (on circle) has direction v, then the other intersection is given by: E = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. That's correct.

Alternatively, we can use the concept of the chord: The reflection of A across the line through the origin perpendicular to the chord? Not.

Maybe we can find a more convenient parametrization: Since we have a line through A, we can write the line equation in terms of its angle. But maybe we can compute the coordinates of E and F using the fact that A, E, and the center are related by the chord. For circle centered at origin, if A is on circle, then the chord AE is such that the origin is the circumcenter, so the line through origin and the midpoint of AE is perpendicular to AE. So the midpoint of AE is the projection of the origin onto the line through A and E? Actually, the perpendicular from the origin to chord AE goes through the midpoint of AE. So the midpoint M_AE is the foot of the perpendicular from origin to line AP. So we can compute M_AE = foot of perpendicular from O to line AP. That foot is given by: M_AE = A + t0 v, where t0 = - (A·v)/|v|^2? Because the projection of A onto the line direction v gives the foot from origin? Wait, the foot of perpendicular from origin to line through A with direction v is the point on line that minimizes distance to origin. That point is given by: A + t0 v, where t0 = - (A·v)/|v|^2. Indeed, because we want (A + t v) ⟂ v? Actually, the condition for foot from origin: (A + t v) is perpendicular to v? No, the vector from origin to point on line is A + t v; we want that vector to be perpendicular to the line direction v, so (A + t v) · v = 0 => A·v + t |v|^2 = 0 => t = - (A·v)/|v|^2. So foot from origin to line is point F0 = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Then the midpoint of AE is that foot? Check: For a chord of a circle centered at origin, the line from origin to the midpoint of chord is perpendicular to chord, so the midpoint is the foot of the perpendicular from origin to the chord line. So yes, midpoint of AE = foot from origin to line AP. So M_AE = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. Then E = 2 M_AE - A = A - 2 (A·v)/|v|^2 v. So consistent.

Similarly, for Γ with center N=(c,0), the midpoint of AF is the foot of perpendicular from N to line AP. So M_AF = A - ((A-c)·v)/|v|^2 v? Actually, foot from N to line: point on line A + t v such that (A + t v - N) · v = 0 => (A-N)·v + t |v|^2 = 0 => t = - (A-N)·v/|v|^2. So M_AF = A - ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2) v. Then F = 2 M_AF - A = A - 2 ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2 v.

So we have expressions for E and F.

Now we want circle through B=(a,-h), E, F. Since E and F are symmetric in some sense? Not symmetric about x-axis because A and B are symmetric. But note that if we reflect the whole configuration across the x-axis, A becomes B, and the line AP becomes a line through B and the reflection of P across x-axis. That reflected line is not AP generally. However, maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is symmetric with respect to the line through H parallel to AP? Not.

Maybe we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) using the fact that it is the circle through B and the two points on line AP. Let's denote the line AP: l. The points E and F are on l. So the circle through B, E, F is the circle through B and the chord EF on l. There is a known formula: The power of a point X on l with respect to the circle is (X-B) times something? Actually, if we consider the circle through B and the two points E and F on l, then for any point X on l, the power of X w.r.t. the circle is (X-E)(X-F) (signed distances along l) if we take the line as a coordinate? But careful: The power of a point on the line l is the product of distances from that point to the two intersection points of the line with the circle. Since the line l intersects the circle at E and F, for any point X on l, the power is (XE)*(XF) (with sign if directed). So for point H? H is not on l generally. But if we want to show that line L through H parallel to l is tangent to the circle, we could consider the pole-polar relationship: The polar of a point on l is the line through the other intersection? Not.

Alternatively, we can use the concept of the radical axis: The line L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the power of H with respect to that circle equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but that's not.

Maybe we can compute the condition that the line through H parallel to l is tangent to the circle by showing that the distance from the circumcenter O to the line equals the radius. That can be expressed as: the power of H with respect to the circle is equal to the square of the distance from H to the point where the line from H perpendicular to l meets the circle? Not.

Let's compute O and radius explicitly. Let's denote coordinates in terms of a,h,c,r,R. We have p = (c+R-r)/2. Also, we have y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). Let’s also compute v = (p-a, y_P - h). For simplicity, let’s compute using the relations. We might also find that t_E and t_F have simple expressions in terms of p and a and c. Let's compute A·v and (A-c)·v.

We have v = (p-a, y_P - h). We can compute A·v = a(p-a) + h(y_P - h) = a(p-a) + h y_P - h^2. But y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). So h y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/2. Also, h^2 = r^2 - a^2. So A·v = a(p-a) - (a+r)(c+R-r)/2 - (r^2 - a^2). Similarly, (A-c)·v = (a-c)(p-a) + h(y_P - h) = (a-c)(p-a) + h y_P - h^2 = (a-c)(p-a) - (a+r)(c+R-r)/2 - (r^2 - a^2). So these are messy.

But perhaps we can find that t_E = (p-a)/something? Alternatively, we can compute E and F directly by solving the line and circle equations. Since we have line AP: we can write its equation in terms of A and P. Let’s find the line's equation in terms of x and y. We have A(a,h) and P(p, y_P). The line can be written as: (y - h)/(x - a) = (y_P - h)/(p - a) = slope m_l. So we can denote m = (y_P - h)/(p - a). Then line: y = h + m (x - a). So y = m x + (h - m a). Now we need intersection with circle x^2+y^2=r^2. Substitute: x^2 + (m x + (h - m a))^2 = r^2. Expand: x^2 + m^2 x^2 + 2m(h - m a)x + (h - m a)^2 = r^2 => (1+m^2)x^2 + 2m(h - m a)x + (h - m a)^2 - r^2 = 0. Since A is on both, x=a is a root. So the quadratic factors as (x - a)( (1+m^2)x + something ) = 0. Let's find the other root x_E. The sum of roots: a + x_E = - [2m(h - m a)]/(1+m^2). So x_E = - [2m(h - m a)]/(1+m^2) - a. Then y_E = m x_E + (h - m a). That gives E coordinates. Similarly, for Γ: (x-c)^2 + y^2 = R^2. Substitute y = m x + (h - m a). Then (x-c)^2 + (m x + (h - m a))^2 = R^2. Expand: (1+m^2)x^2 + 2(m(h - m a) - c? Actually compute: (x-c)^2 = x^2 - 2cx + c^2. So sum: x^2 - 2cx + c^2 + m^2 x^2 + 2m(h - m a)x + (h - m a)^2 = (1+m^2)x^2 + (2m(h - m a) - 2c)x + (c^2 + (h - m a)^2) = R^2. So equation: (1+m^2)x^2 + 2(m(h - m a) - c)x + (c^2 + (h - m a)^2 - R^2) = 0. Since A is on Γ, x=a is a root. So the other root x_F =? Sum of roots: a + x_F = -2(m(h - m a) - c)/(1+m^2). So x_F = -2(m(h - m a) - c)/(1+m^2) - a. Then y_F = m x_F + (h - m a). So we have explicit coordinates for E and F in terms of a, h, m, c. But m is slope of AP. We can also compute m in terms of a,h,c,r,R. Since we have P = (p, y_P) with p = (c+R-r)/2, y_P = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h). Then m = (y_P - h)/(p - a). That might simplify using the relations.

Let's compute m. We have p - a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. And y_P - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h = -(a+r)(c+R-r)/(2h) - h. Combine over 2h: = -( (a+r)(c+R-r) + 2h^2 )/(2h). But 2h^2 = 2(r^2 - a^2) = 2(r-a)(r+a). So numerator = (a+r)(c+R-r) + 2(r+a)(r-a) = (a+r)[c+R-r + 2(r-a)] = (a+r)[c+R+r-2a]. So y_P - h = -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h). So m = [ -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h) ] / [ (c+R-r)/2 - a ] = [ -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h) ] / [ (c+R-r - 2a)/2 ] = [ -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a)/(2h) ] * [2/(c+R-r-2a)] = -(a+r)(c+R+r-2a) / [ h (c+R-r-2a) ].

So m = - (a+r)(c+R+r-2a) / [ h (c+R-r-2a) ].

Now note that c+R-r-2a = (c+R-r) - 2a = 2p - 2a = 2(p-a). So denominator = h * 2(p-a). So m = - (a+r)(c+R+r-2a) / (2h(p-a)). But we also have c+R+r-2a =? Let's compute: c+R+r-2a = (c+R-r-2a) + 2r? Not. Actually, we can express c+R+r-2a = (c+R-r-2a) + 2r = 2(p-a) + 2r = 2(p-a+r). So m = - (a+r) * 2(p-a+r) / (2h(p-a)) = - (a+r)(p-a+r) / [ h (p-a) ].

Thus m = - (a+r)(p-a+r) / [ h (p-a) ].

Now we have m expressed in terms of a, h, p, r. But we also have relation between a, h, r, p? Since p = (c+R-r)/2, and a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). Not simple.

Maybe we can find a relation between p-a and something. Note that p-a = (c+R-r)/2 - a. And we also have a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c). Now also compute p-a+r =? p-a+r = (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c) + r = ( (R-r)(c+R+r) + 2cr )/(2c) = ( (R-r)(c+R+r) + 2cr )/(2c). Expand (R-r)(c+R+r) = (R-r)c + (R-r)(R+r) = c(R-r) + (R^2 - r^2). Now also compute a+r? a+r =? We have a = (c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c), so a+r = (c^2+r^2-R^2+2cr)/(2c) = ((c+r)^2 - R^2)/(2c) = (c+r-R)(c+r+R)/(2c). So a+r = ((c+r-R)(c+r+R))/(2c).

Now we have m = - (a+r)(p-a+r) / [ h (p-a) ] = - [ ((c+r-R)(c+r+R))/(2c) ] * [ p (R+r)/c ] / [ h * p (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c) ]? Actually, p-a = (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c). So h(p-a) = h (R-r)(c+R+r)/(2c). And (a+r)(p-a+r) = ((c+r-R)(c+r+R)/(2c)) * (p (R+r)/c) = p (R+r)(c+r-R)(c+r+R)/(2c^2). Simplify: (c+r+R) = (c+R+r). So numerator: p (R+r)(c+r-R)(c+R+r). Denominator: c h (R-r)(c+R+r). Cancel (c+R+r): m = - p (R+r)(c+r-R) / [ c h (R-r) ].

Now note that c+r-R =? Since R>r, c+r-R could be positive or negative depending on c. In our numeric, c=1, r=5, R=5.83, so c+r-R=1+5-5.83=0.17, positive. So m = - p (R+r)(c+r-R) / [ c h (R-r) ].

Now we also have p = (c+R-r)/2. So m = - (c+R-r)/2 * (R+r)(c+r-R) / [ c h (R-r) ] = - (c+R-r)(R+r)(c+r-R) / [ 2 c h (R-r) ].

This is a nice expression for the slope m. Now we also have h^2 = r^2 - a^2. But we can also find h in terms of c,r,R? Using the formula: h^2 = r^2 - ((c^2+r^2-R^2)/(2c))^2. That is standard. Now we have expressions for E and F in terms of a, h, m, c. But we also have a and h expressed in terms of c,r,R. Perhaps we can compute the circumcircle of BEF in a nicer way. Since we have explicit coordinates for E and F, we can compute the circle equation. Let's do that.

We have line l: y = m x + b, where b = h - m a. So l: y = m x + (h - m a). Also, we have B = (a, -h). Now E and F are points on l with x-coordinates x_E and x_F as derived. We can find the equation of circle through B, E, F. Since E and F lie on l, the circle's equation can be expressed as: (x^2+y^2) + u x + v y + w = 0. But perhaps we can find that the center O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of BE and the perpendicular bisector of BF. Alternatively, we can use the fact that for a chord on l, the circle is determined by the condition that the power of a point on l is product of distances. For point H (not on l), the condition that line through H parallel to l is tangent to the circle can be expressed in terms of the polar. In fact, if we take the line through H parallel to l, then the condition that this line is tangent to the circle is equivalent to the condition that the pole of H with respect to the circle lies on l? Not exactly.

Let's think about the geometry: We have a circle (BEF) and a line L through H parallel to l. L is tangent to the circle if and only if the line through H perpendicular to l meets the circle at a point that is the point of tangency, and the distance from H to that point is the tangent length. That is not simple.

Given the difficulty, I think an analytic solution is the most straightforward to ensure rigor. We have derived expressions for x_E, x_F, and also for y_E, y_F. We can compute the circumcircle of BEF using the fact that points E and F lie on line l: y = αx + h. And B is (0,-h). We can find the equation of the circle through B and the two points on l. There is a known formula: If we have a line l and a point B not on l, then the family of circles through B and intersecting l at two points can be expressed as: (x^2+y^2) + Ux + Vy + W = 0 with the condition that the line l satisfies something. x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2) - a.
x_F = 2(d - α b)/(1+α^2) - a.

Now, note that b = h - α a. So α b = α h - α^2 a. Then 2α b/(1+α^2) = 2(α h - α^2 a)/(1+α^2) = 2α h/(1+α^2) - 2α^2 a/(1+α^2). So x_E = - [2α h/(1+α^2) - 2α^2 a/(1+α^2)] - a = -2α h/(1+α^2) + 2α^2 a/(1+α^2) - a = -2α h/(1+α^2) + a(2α^2/(1+α^2) - 1) = -2α h/(1+α^2) + a( (2α^2 - (1+α^2))/(1+α^2) ) = -2α h/(1+α^2) + a( (α^2 - 1)/(1+α^2) ) = -2α h/(1+α^2) - a(1-α^2)/(1+α^2). So x_E = - [2α h + a(1-α^2)]/(1+α^2). But note that a(1-α^2) = a - aα^2. So x_E = - (a + 2α h - aα^2)/(1+α^2). This is not symmetric.

Similarly, x_F = 2(d - α b)/(1+α^2) - a = 2(d - α(h - α a))/(1+α^2) - a = 2(d - α h + α^2 a)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2α h + 2α^2 a)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2α h + 2α^2 a - a(1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2α h + 2α^2 a - a - aα^2)/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2α h + α^2 a - a)/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2α h - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). So x_F = (2d - 2α h - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2).

Now, we also have y_E = α x_E + b = α x_E + h - α a = α(x_E - a) + h. But x_E - a = -2α b/(1+α^2) - 2a? Actually, from x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2) - a, so x_E - a = -2α b/(1+α^2) - 2a. Then y_E = -2α^2 b/(1+α^2) - 2α a + α a + b = -2α^2 b/(1+α^2) - α a + b = b - α a - 2α^2 b/(1+α^2) = (b(1 - 2α^2/(1+α^2))) - α a = b( (1+α^2 - 2α^2)/(1+α^2) ) - α a = b( (1-α^2)/(1+α^2) ) - α a. But b = h - α a, so y_E = (h - α a)(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a[ (1-α^2)/(1+α^2) + 1 ] = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a[ (1-α^2 + 1+α^2)/(1+α^2) ] = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - α a[ 2/(1+α^2) ] = [ h(1-α^2) - 2α a ]/(1+α^2). Given the time, I'll try to compute using the determinant formula for the circumcenter of BEF. We have expressions for x_E, y_E, x_F, y_F in terms of a, h, α, b, d. But we also have relations that simplify these expressions. Let's try to find simpler expressions for x_E and x_F using the fact that E and F are the second intersections of line l with circles Ω and Γ. There is a known formula: For a line through A with slope α, the x-coordinate of the other intersection with circle centered at origin is given by: x_E = - (a(1-α^2) + 2αh)/(1+α^2)? Actually, from the quadratic: (1+α^2)x^2 + 2αb x + (b^2 - r^2)=0. Since x=a is a root, we have (1+α^2)a^2 + 2αb a + b^2 - r^2 = 0. So the other root x_E satisfies: a + x_E = -2αb/(1+α^2). So x_E = -2αb/(1+α^2) - a. So we need to compute -2αb/(1+α^2). But we also have b = h - αa. So -2αb = -2α(h - αa) = -2αh + 2α^2a. So x_E = (-2αh + 2α^2a)/(1+α^2) - a = -2αh/(1+α^2) + a(2α^2/(1+α^2) - 1) = -2αh/(1+α^2) + a((2α^2 - (1+α^2))/(1+α^2)) = -2αh/(1+α^2) + a(α^2-1)/(1+α^2) = - (2αh + a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). So x_E = - (2αh + a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). Similarly, x_F = 2(d - αb)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2αb)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2α(h - αa))/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2αh + 2α^2a)/(1+α^2) - a = (2d - 2αh + 2α^2a - a(1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = (2d - 2αh - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2). So x_F = (2d - 2αh - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2).

Now, we also have y_E = α x_E + b = α x_E + h - αa = α(x_E - a) + h. And x_E - a = - (2αh + a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2) - a = - (2αh + a(1-α^2) + a(1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = - (2αh + a(1-α^2+1+α^2))/(1+α^2) = - (2αh + 2a)/(1+α^2) = -2(αh + a)/(1+α^2). So y_E = α * (-2(αh+a)/(1+α^2)) + h = -2α^2h/(1+α^2) -2αa/(1+α^2) + h = h(1 - 2α^2/(1+α^2)) - 2αa/(1+α^2) = h((1+α^2-2α^2)/(1+α^2)) - 2αa/(1+α^2) = h(1-α^2)/(1+α^2) - 2αa/(1+α^2) = [h(1-α^2) - 2αa]/(1+α^2). So y_E = (h(1-α^2) - 2αa)/(1+α^2). Good.

Similarly, y_F = α x_F + b = α * (2d - 2αh - a(1-α^2))/(1+α^2) + h - αa = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa(1-α^2)]/(1+α^2) + h - αa = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a]/(1+α^2) + h - αa. Write h - αa = (h(1+α^2) - αa(1+α^2))/(1+α^2). So y_F = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a + h(1+α^2) - αa(1+α^2)]/(1+α^2) = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a + h + hα^2 - αa - α^3a]/(1+α^2) = [2αd - 2α^2h - αa + α^3a + h + hα^2 - αa - α^3a]/(1+α^2) = [2αd + h + hα^2 - 2α^2h - 2αa]/(1+α^2) = [2αd + h(1+α^2-2α^2) - 2αa]/(1+α^2) = [2αd + h(1-α^2) - 2αa]/(1+α^2). So y_F = (2αd + h(1-α^2) - 2αa)/(1+α^2). This is consistent with earlier.

Given the time, I'll attempt a computational proof with the help of some algebraic simplifications. Let's set up the problem in a coordinate system that simplifies the expressions for E and F. - P = (p, 0) with p > 0 (since AP is a radius, P is the center of ω, and A is on ω, so the other intersection of AP with ω is A' = (2p, 0) because P is the midpoint of AA'? So A is at (0,0), and the other intersection with x-axis is (2p,0). So A'=(2p,0). Good.

- The line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. Since A is at origin, let B = (b_x, b_y). Then the midpoint of AB is (b_x/2, b_y/2). The line MN is perpendicular to AB, so its direction is perpendicular to vector (b_x, b_y). So its equation: (x - b_x/2, y - b_y/2) · (b_x, b_y) = 0 => b_x(x - b_x/2) + b_y(y - b_y/2) = 0 => b_x x + b_y y = (b_x^2+b_y^2)/2 = |B|^2/2. So MN: b_x x + b_y y = |B|^2/2. Since B lies on both circles, we have that M and N are points on MN such that the circles pass through A and B. Also, M and N are the centers of Ω and Γ. Their distances to A are r and R, so MA = r, NA = R. Also, M and N are on MN. So M and N are the points on MN such that the distance from M to A is r, and from M to B is also r. So M is the intersection of MN with the perpendicular bisector of AB? Actually, the perpendicular bisector of AB is MN itself, so M lies on that line. So M is the point on MN that is at distance r from A. So M is the point on MN such that MA = r. Similarly, N is the point on MN such that NA = R. So M and N are the points on MN that satisfy that the distances to A are given. Since MN is a line, we can param M and N as points on that line. Let's denote the line MN: L: b_x x + b_y y = |B|^2/2. Let’s also denote the distance from A to any point X on L. The set of points X on L such that XA = r is the intersection of L with the circle centered at A radius r. That is a circle with center A radius r. So M is one of the intersection points of L with that circle. Similarly, N is one of the intersection points of L with the circle centered at A radius R. So M and N are determined by the geometry of A and the line L.

Now, we also have points C and D on L such that C, M, N, D are collinear in that order. And C is on Ω (circle centered at M through A), D is on Γ (circle centered at N through A). So C and D are the points on L such that the distances to M and N are r and R respectively, and they are on the same line L. But note that A is also on both circles, but A is not on L generally. So C is the second intersection of L with circle Ω. Similarly, D is the second intersection of L with circle Γ.

Thus, we have a configuration: Line L (MN) is the perpendicular bisector of AB. On L, we have points M and N (centers) and also points C and D (on circles). And we have the circle ω (center P) passing through A, C, D. So ω is the circle with diameter? Not.

Since ω is a circle through A, and L is some line, the chord CD of ω on L has its midpoint at the foot of the perpendicular from P to L. That foot is Q, the projection of P onto L. So Q is the midpoint of CD. Also, since A is on ω, and A is not on L generally, the line L will intersect ω at two points: C and D. So C and D are the two intersections. Now, we also have that M and N are points on L such that MA = r and NA = R, and also M is the center of Ω that passes through A and C, and N is the center of Γ that passes through A and D. So if we find the foot Q from A to L, then B = 2Q. The foot Q is given by: Q = ( -m l/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) )? Actually, the foot of perpendicular from origin to line y = lx + m is: ( -m l/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). Because the line's equation: lx - y + m = 0. The foot from origin is ( -A C/(A^2+B^2) )? Standard formula: For line ax+by+c=0, foot from (x0,y0) is (x0 - a*(ax0+by0+c)/(a^2+b^2), y0 - b*(ax0+by0+c)/(a^2+b^2)). Here, a=l, b=-1, c=m. So ax0+by0+c = 0 + 0 + m = m. So foot Q = ( -l*m/(l^2+1), -(-1)*m/(l^2+1) ) = ( -l m/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). So Q = ( -l m/(1+l^2), m/(1+l^2) ). Then B = 2Q = ( -2l m/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2) ). So B is determined by l and m.

Now, C and D are the intersections of L with ω. Substitute y = lx + m into ω: x^2 + (lx+m)^2 = 2px => x^2 + l^2 x^2 + 2l m x + m^2 = 2px => (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - p)x + m^2 = 0. This quadratic gives the x-coordinates of C and D. Let the roots be x_C and x_D. Then the product x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2). The sum x_C + x_D = -2(l m - p)/(1+l^2) = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2). So we have x_C and x_D.

Now, we also have that M is the circumcenter of A and C. Since A=0, the circumcenter of A and C is the midpoint of the perpendicular bisector of AC. But we can also find M as the point on L such that MA = MC. Since A is origin, MA = |M|. And MC = |M - C|. So condition: |M| = |M - C|. Squaring: |M|^2 = |M|^2 - 2 M·C + |C|^2 => 2 M·C = |C|^2 => M·C = |C|^2/2. So M is the point on L such that the dot product with C equals half of |C|^2. Since M lies on L, we can param M as (t, l t + m). Then M·C = t x_C + (l t + m) y_C = t x_C + l t y_C + m y_C = t (x_C + l y_C) + m y_C. But note that y_C = l x_C + m, so x_C + l y_C = x_C + l(l x_C + m) = x_C + l^2 x_C + l m = (1+l^2)x_C + l m. So M·C = t[(1+l^2)x_C + l m] + m y_C. And |C|^2 = x_C^2 + y_C^2. So we have t[(1+l^2)x_C + l m] + m y_C = (x_C^2 + y_C^2)/2. This gives t. So M is determined by C.

Similarly, N is the point on L such that N·D = |D|^2/2.

Now, we also have that E = (2M_x, 0) and F = (2N_x, 0). So E and F are points on the x-axis.

t x_C + l t y_C + m y_C = t (x_C + l y_C) + m y_C = t (x_C + l(l x_C+m)) + m y_C = t (x_C + l^2 x_C + l m) + m y_C = t ((1+l^2)x_C + l m) + m y_C.

Set equal to (x_C^2+y_C^2)/2. So t = [ (x_C^2+y_C^2)/2 - m y_C ] / [ (1+l^2)x_C + l m ].

But note that x_C and y_C satisfy the circle equation: x_C^2+y_C^2 = 2p x_C. So (x_C^2+y_C^2)/2 = p x_C. So t = [ p x_C - m y_C ] / [ (1+l^2)x_C + l m ].

Thus, M_x = t = (p x_C - m y_C) / ( (1+l^2)x_C + l m ). Similarly, we can compute M_y = l t + m = l (p x_C - m y_C)/( (1+l^2)x_C + l m ) + m.

Given the time, I think we can try to compute H explicitly using the formula for orthocenter in terms of coordinates. Let's denote:

P = (p,0)
M = (M_x, M_y)
N = (N_x, N_y)

We can compute the orthocenter H as:

H = ( x_H, y_H ) such that:

(1) (H - P) · (M - N) = 0 (since altitude from P is perpendicular to MN)
(2) (H - M) · (P - N) = 0 (altitude from M is perpendicular to PN)

So we have:

(H_x - p, H_y) · (M_x - N_x, M_y - N_y) = 0  => (H_x - p)(M_x - N_x) + H_y (M_y - N_y) = 0
(H_x - M_x, H_y - M_y) · (P_x - N_x, P_y - N_y) = 0 => (H_x - M_x)(p - N_x) + (H_y - M_y)(-N_y) = 0

We can solve for H_x, H_y. This is a linear system. We can write:

(1) (M_x - N_x) H_x + (M_y - N_y) H_y = p (M_x - N_x)
(2) (p - N_x) H_x - N_y H_y = M_x (p - N_x) + M_y N_y

Actually, from second: (H_x - M_x)(p - N_x) + (H_y - M_y)(-N_y)=0 => (p - N_x) H_x - N_y H_y = M_x (p - N_x) + M_y N_y.

So we have:

A H_x + B H_y = C
D H_x + E H_y = F

where A = M_x - N_x, B = M_y - N_y, C = p (M_x - N_x)
D = p - N_x, E = -N_y, F = M_x (p - N_x) + M_y N_y.

Then we can solve for H_y. The solution will involve M and N.

Now, we also have that the horizontal line through H is y = H_y. We need to show that this line is tangent to circle through B, E, F. That circle's equation can be found using coordinates. Since E and F are on x-axis, we can find the circle's equation by using the fact that the circle passes through B, (2M_x,0), (2N_x,0). So we can write the circle's equation as: x^2+y^2 + Ux + Vy + W=0. Since it passes through (2M_x,0): (2M_x)^2 + U(2M_x) + W = 0 => 4M_x^2 + 2U M_x + W = 0. Similarly, for (2N_x,0): 4N_x^2 + 2U N_x + W = 0. Subtracting: 4(M_x^2 - N_x^2) + 2U(M_x - N_x) = 0 => 2(M_x - N_x)(2(M_x+N_x) + U) = 0 => U = -2(M_x+N_x). Then from the equation with M_x: 4M_x^2 + 2(-2(M_x+N_x)) M_x + W = 4M_x^2 -4M_x(M_x+N_x)+W = 4M_x^2 -4M_x^2 -4M_x N_x + W = -4M_x N_x + W = 0 => W = 4M_x N_x.

Now, the circle also passes through B = (b_x, b_y). So b_x^2+b_y^2 + U b_x + V b_y + W = 0 => (b_x^2+b_y^2) -2(M_x+N_x) b_x + V b_y + 4M_x N_x = 0 => V b_y = - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x. Thus, V = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y.

Now, the center O = (-U/2, -V/2) = (M_x+N_x, -V/2). The radius R_circ = sqrt( (U^2+V^2)/4 - W ) = sqrt( (4(M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2)/4 - 4M_x N_x ) = sqrt( (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x )? Actually, careful: U = -2(M_x+N_x), so U^2/4 = (4(M_x+N_x)^2)/4 = (M_x+N_x)^2. Then R^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x.

Now, we need to show that the horizontal line y = H_y is tangent to this circle. The distance from O to the line is |y_O - H_y| = | -V/2 - H_y | = | - (V/2 + H_y) | = | V/2 + H_y |. So we need to show that | V/2 + H_y | = R_circ. That is: (V/2 + H_y)^2 = R^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x.

Expand left: V^2/4 + V H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 + V^2/4 - 4M_x N_x => Cancel V^2/4: V H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4M_x N_x = M_x^2+2M_x N_x+N_x^2 -4M_x N_x = M_x^2 -2M_x N_x + N_x^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2.

So we need to show: V H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2. That is: H_y (V + H_y) = (M_x - N_x)^2. But note that V + H_y =? We have V = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y. So V + H_y = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y + H_y. So the condition is:

H_y * ( [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y + H_y ) = (M_x - N_x)^2.

Multiply both sides by b_y: H_y ( - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x + H_y b_y ) = (M_x - N_x)^2 b_y.

So: H_y ( - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ) + H_y^2 b_y = (M_x - N_x)^2 b_y.

Thus: H_y ( - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ) = b_y ( (M_x - N_x)^2 - H_y^2 ).

So we need to show that H_y satisfies this equation. This is a relation that can be verified by substituting the expressions for M_x, N_x, b_x, b_y, H_y in terms of l, m, p. So it's a heavy algebraic identity, but it's doable.

Now, also note that from the circle equation, we have x_C^2+y_C^2 = 2p x_C. So (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2l m x_C + m^2 = 2p x_C => (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2(l m - p)x_C + m^2 =0. So we can use that.

Now, we also have that M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/((1+l^2)x_C + l m), with K = p - l m. So we can compute l M_x + m = l(K x_C - m^2)/((1+l^2)x_C + l m) + m = [ lK x_C - l m^2 + m((1+l^2)x_C + l m) ] / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = [ lK x_C - l m^2 + m(1+l^2)x_C + l m^2 ] / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = [ lK x_C + m(1+l^2)x_C ] / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = x_C ( lK + m(1+l^2) ) / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). Now, K = p - l m. So lK = l p - l^2 m. Then lK + m(1+l^2) = l p - l^2 m + m + l^2 m = l p + m. So l M_x + m = x_C (l p + m) / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). So M_y = l M_x + m = (l p + m) x_C / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). That's a nice expression.

Similarly, N_y = l N_x + m = (l p + m) x_D / ((1+l^2)x_D + l m).

Thus, we have:

M_y = (l p + m) x_C / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)
N_y = (l p + m) x_D / ((1+l^2)x_D + l m)

Also, p - M_x = (l p + m) y_C / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = (l p + m) (l x_C + m) / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)

Now, compute l N_x + m = N_y = (l p + m) x_D / ((1+l^2)x_D + l m). And l M_x + m = M_y = (l p + m) x_C / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). So the product (l N_x + m)(l M_x + m) = (l p + m)^2 x_C x_D / [ ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ].

Now, note that (p - M_x)(p - N_x) = (l p + m)^2 y_C y_D / [ ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ] = (l p + m)^2 (l x_C + m)(l x_D + m) / [ ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ].

Thus, numerator of H_y = (p - M_x)(p - N_x) + (l N_x + m)(l M_x + m) = (l p + m)^2 [ (l x_C + m)(l x_D + m) + x_C x_D ] / [ ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ].

Now, compute the bracket: (l x_C + m)(l x_D + m) + x_C x_D = l^2 x_C x_D + l m (x_C + x_D) + m^2 + x_C x_D = (l^2+1) x_C x_D + l m (x_C + x_D) + m^2.

But from the quadratic, we have (1+l^2)x_C x_D = m^2? Actually, x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2). So (l^2+1) x_C x_D = m^2. So the bracket becomes: m^2 + l m (x_C + x_D) + m^2 = 2m^2 + l m (x_C + x_D). But x_C + x_D = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2). So bracket = 2m^2 + l m * 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2) = 2m^2 + 2l m(p - l m)/(1+l^2) = 2m^2 (1+l^2)/(1+l^2) + 2l m(p - l m)/(1+l^2) = 2[ m^2(1+l^2) + l m(p - l m) ]/(1+l^2) = 2[ m^2 + l^2 m^2 + l m p - l^2 m^2 ]/(1+l^2) = 2[ m^2 + l m p ]/(1+l^2) = 2m(m + l p)/(1+l^2). So bracket = 2m(l p + m)/(1+l^2).

Thus, numerator = (l p + m)^2 * [ 2m(l p + m)/(1+l^2) ] / [ ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ] = 2m(l p + m)^3 / [ (1+l^2) ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ].

Now, denominator of H_y = l p + m. So:

H_y = [ 2m(l p + m)^3 / ( (1+l^2) ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ) ] / (l p + m) = 2m(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2) ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ].

Thus, H_y = 2m(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2) ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) ].

Now, we also have that ((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = x_C + l y_C =? We can compute the product ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m). Let's denote D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m, D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + l m. Then their product is? We can compute using the quadratic. Since x_C and x_D are roots, we have:

(1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - p)x + m^2 = 0. So (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(l m - p)x + m^2 = 0. For x = x_C, we have (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2(l m - p)x_C + m^2 = 0 => (1+l^2)x_C^2 + l m x_C = p x_C - m^2. Not sure.

Alternatively, we can compute D_C * D_D = ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m) = (1+l^2)^2 x_C x_D + l m (1+l^2)(x_C + x_D) + l^2 m^2. Now, we know x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2), and x_C + x_D = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2). So compute:

(1+l^2)^2 * (m^2/(1+l^2)) = (1+l^2) m^2.
l m (1+l^2) * (2(p - l m)/(1+l^2)) = l m * 2(p - l m) = 2l m(p - l m).
l^2 m^2 = l^2 m^2.

So D_C D_D = (1+l^2)m^2 + 2l m(p - l m) + l^2 m^2 = m^2 + l^2 m^2 + 2l m p - 2l^2 m^2 + l^2 m^2 = m^2 + 2l m p. So D_C D_D = m(m + 2l p). Wait, check: m^2 + 2l m p = m(m + 2l p). Yes.

Thus, product = m(m+2l p). That's nice!

Therefore, H_y = 2m(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2) * m(m+2l p) ] = 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ].

So H_y = 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ].

Now, note that m+2l p =? Since we have l p + m, and m+2l p = (l p + m) + l p =? Not important.

Now, we also have that the horizontal line through H is y = H_y. We need to show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). The circle (BEF) has center O = (M_x+N_x, -V/2) and radius R_circ. The condition we derived earlier is: H_y (V + H_y) = (M_x - N_x)^2. Or equivalently, H_y (V + H_y) = (M_x - N_x)^2. Since we have expressions for M_x, N_x, and V, we can verify this identity.

We have:

M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/(D_C) with K = p - l m, D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m.
N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/(D_D).

We also have B = (b_x, b_y) with b_x = -2l m/(1+l^2), b_y = 2m/(1+l^2). And V = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y.

We can try to compute V + H_y. Perhaps there is a simpler relation: The circle (BEF) might be the circle with diameter from B to the point (M_x+N_x, 0)? Not.

Given the time, I think we can try to verify the condition H_y (V + H_y) = (M_x - N_x)^2 by substituting the known expressions. Let's compute M_x - N_x and (M_x - N_x)^2 in terms of the roots. Since we have M_x and N_x, we can compute:

M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C, N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/D_D.

We also have D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m, D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + l m. And K = p - l m.

Now, note that from the circle equation, we have (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2(l m - p)x_C + m^2 = 0. This can be written as: (1+l^2)x_C^2 + l m x_C = p x_C - m^2. So p x_C - m^2 = (1+l^2)x_C^2 + l m x_C = x_C((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = x_C D_C. So p x_C - m^2 = x_C D_C. Thus, K x_C - m^2 = (p - l m)x_C - m^2 = p x_C - l m x_C - m^2 = (p x_C - m^2) - l m x_C = x_C D_C - l m x_C = x_C (D_C - l m). But D_C - l m = (1+l^2)x_C + l m - l m = (1+l^2)x_C. So K x_C - m^2 = x_C (1+l^2)x_C = (1+l^2)x_C^2. That's a very nice simplification! Indeed:

From p x_C - m^2 = x_C D_C, we have K x_C - m^2 = p x_C - l m x_C - m^2 = (p x_C - m^2) - l m x_C = x_C D_C - l m x_C = x_C (D_C - l m) = x_C ((1+l^2)x_C + l m - l m) = (1+l^2)x_C^2.

So M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). Similarly, N_x = (1+l^2)x_D^2 / D_D.

Thus, M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). And N_x = (1+l^2)x_D^2 / ((1+l^2)x_D + l m). This is much simpler.

Now, we can compute M_x - N_x. Also, we can compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x.

Now, note that D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m. So we have:

M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C. Similarly, N_x = (1+l^2)x_D^2 / D_D.

Now, we can also compute b_x, b_y. b_x = -2l m/(1+l^2), b_y = 2m/(1+l^2). So b_x^2+b_y^2 = (4l^2 m^2 + 4m^2)/(1+l^2)^2 = 4m^2(l^2+1)/(1+l^2)^2 = 4m^2/(1+l^2). So (b_x^2+b_y^2) = 4m^2/(1+l^2). Also, note that b_x = -2l m/(1+l^2), so 2(M_x+N_x) b_x = 2(M_x+N_x) * (-2l m/(1+l^2)) = -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2). And 4M_x N_x is there.

Now, we need V = [ - (b_x^2+b_y^2) + 2(M_x+N_x) b_x - 4M_x N_x ] / b_y. So:

V = [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] / (2m/(1+l^2)) = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4m^2/(1+l^2) -4l m (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4/(1+l^2) ( m^2 + l m (M_x+N_x) ) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * [ -4/(1+l^2) ( m^2 + l m (M_x+N_x) ) - 4M_x N_x ] = (1+l^2)/(2m) * ( -4/(1+l^2) ( m^2 + l m (M_x+N_x) ) ) - (1+l^2)/(2m) * 4M_x N_x = -2/(m) ( m^2 + l m (M_x+N_x) ) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x.

Thus, V = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x.

Now, we also have H_y = 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ].

Now, we need to compute V + H_y. That is: V + H_y = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x + H_y.

Now, we also need (M_x - N_x)^2. Let's compute M_x - N_x and (M_x - N_x)^2 in terms of x_C, x_D.

We have M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C, with D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m. Similarly, N_x = (1+l^2)x_D^2 / D_D. So M_x - N_x = (1+l^2) [ x_C^2/D_C - x_D^2/D_D ].

Compute the common denominator: D_C D_D = m(m+2l p) as derived? Actually, we had D_C D_D = m(m+2l p). But we need to be careful: we derived D_C D_D = m(m+2l p). But is that correct? Let's re-derive: D_C D_D = ((1+l^2)x_C + l m)((1+l^2)x_D + l m). We computed that using x_C x_D and x_C + x_D. We got D_C D_D = m^2 + 2l m p = m(m+2l p). Yes, that is correct.

So D_C D_D = m(m+2l p). Now, note that m+2l p = (l p + m) + l p. But we can keep.

Now, compute x_C^2/D_C - x_D^2/D_D = (x_C^2 D_D - x_D^2 D_C)/(D_C D_D). So we need to compute numerator N = x_C^2 D_D - x_D^2 D_C.

Recall that D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m, D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + l m. So:

N = x_C^2((1+l^2)x_D + l m) - x_D^2((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = (1+l^2)(x_C^2 x_D - x_D^2 x_C) + l m (x_C^2 - x_D^2) = (1+l^2)x_C x_D (x_C - x_D) + l m (x_C - x_D)(x_C + x_D) = (x_C - x_D)[ (1+l^2)x_C x_D + l m (x_C + x_D) ].

Now, we have x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2), and x_C + x_D = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2). So the bracket = (1+l^2)* (m^2/(1+l^2)) + l m * (2(p - l m)/(1+l^2)) = m^2 + 2l m (p - l m)/(1+l^2) = m^2 + 2l m p/(1+l^2) - 2l^2 m^2/(1+l^2) = m^2(1+l^2)/(1+l^2) + 2l m p/(1+l^2) - 2l^2 m^2/(1+l^2) = m^2/(1+l^2) + 2l m p/(1+l^2) = (m^2+2l m p)/(1+l^2) = m(m+2l p)/(1+l^2). So bracket = m(m+2l p)/(1+l^2).

Thus, N = (x_C - x_D) * m(m+2l p)/(1+l^2). Then x_C^2/D_C - x_D^2/D_D = N / (D_C D_D) = (x_C - x_D) * m(m+2l p)/(1+l^2) / (m(m+2l p)) = (x_C - x_D)/(1+l^2). So M_x - N_x = (1+l^2) * (x_C - x_D)/(1+l^2) = x_C - x_D. That's a beautiful simplification! So M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D.

Thus, (M_x - N_x)^2 = (x_C - x_D)^2.

Now, we also need (M_x - N_x)^2 = (x_C - x_D)^2. And we have H_y = 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ].

Now, we need to show that H_y (V + H_y) = (x_C - x_D)^2. But we have V = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x. And M_x+N_x and M_x N_x can be expressed in terms of x_C, x_D. Let's compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x using the simplified forms.

We have M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C, N_x = (1+l^2)x_D^2 / D_D. And D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m, D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + l m.

Now, we also have from earlier: p x_C - m^2 = x_C D_C. And p x_D - m^2 = x_D D_D. So p x_C - m^2 = x_C D_C. That gives p x_C - m^2 = x_C((1+l^2)x_C + l m) = (1+l^2)x_C^2 + l m x_C. So p x_C = (1+l^2)x_C^2 + l m x_C + m^2. So (1+l^2)x_C^2 = p x_C - l m x_C - m^2 = x_C(p - l m) - m^2 = K x_C - m^2. That's consistent.

Now, we can also compute M_x in terms of x_C: M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). This is the same as before.

Now, we need M_x+N_x. Maybe we can compute M_x+N_x = (1+l^2)[ x_C^2/D_C + x_D^2/D_D ].

We can compute this sum using the fact that we have expressions for the sum of the fractions. Alternatively, we can use the relationship we found earlier: (p - M_x) = (l p + m) y_C / D_C, and similarly for N_x. But we might not need explicit M_x+N_x.

We need to compute V + H_y. Let's compute V + H_y = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x + H_y.

We also need (x_C - x_D)^2 = (x_C + x_D)^2 - 4x_C x_D.

We know x_C + x_D = 2(p - l m)/(1+l^2), x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2). So (x_C - x_D)^2 = 4(p - l m)^2/(1+l^2)^2 - 4m^2/(1+l^2) = 4[ (p - l m)^2 - m^2(1+l^2) ]/(1+l^2)^2 = 4[ p^2 - 2l m p + l^2 m^2 - m^2 - l^2 m^2 ]/(1+l^2)^2 = 4(p^2 - 2l m p - m^2)/(1+l^2)^2 = 4(p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p)/(1+l^2)^2.

So (x_C - x_D)^2 = 4(p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p)/(1+l^2)^2.

Now, H_y = 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ].

So H_y = 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ].

Now, we need to show that H_y (V + H_y) = (x_C - x_D)^2. That is: V H_y + H_y^2 = (x_C - x_D)^2.

So V H_y = (x_C - x_D)^2 - H_y^2.

Thus, V = [ (x_C - x_D)^2 - H_y^2 ] / H_y.

Now, we have an expression for V in terms of M_x, N_x. So we need to verify that:

-2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x = [ (x_C - x_D)^2 - H_y^2 ] / H_y.

This is an algebraic identity. Given the simplifications we have, it might be true. We can try to compute the right-hand side in terms of l, m, p.

First, compute H_y^2: H_y^2 = 4(l p + m)^4 / [ (1+l^2)^2 (m+2l p)^2 ].

Then (x_C - x_D)^2 = 4(p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p)/(1+l^2)^2. So:

(x_C - x_D)^2 - H_y^2 = 4/(1+l^2)^2 [ (p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p) - (l p + m)^4 / ( (1+l^2) (m+2l p)^2 ) ].

Then the right-hand side divided by H_y is: [ (x_C - x_D)^2 - H_y^2 ] / H_y = [ 4/(1+l^2)^2 ( ... ) ] / ( 2(l p + m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2l p) ] ) = 4/(1+l^2)^2 * ( ... ) * (1+l^2)(m+2l p) / (2(l p + m)^2) = 2/(1+l^2) * ( ... ) * (m+2l p) / (l p + m)^2.

So V = [ 2(m+2l p) / ((1+l^2)(l p + m)^2) ] * [ (p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p) - (l p + m)^4 / ( (1+l^2) (m+2l p)^2 ) ].

Thus, V = 2(m+2l p)/((1+l^2)(l p + m)^2) * (p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p) - 2(m+2l p)/((1+l^2)(l p + m)^2) * (l p + m)^4 / ( (1+l^2) (m+2l p)^2 ) = 2(m+2l p)(p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p)/((1+l^2)(l p + m)^2) - 2/(1+l^2)^2 * (l p + m)^2 / (m+2l p).

So V = 2(m+2l p)(p^2 - m^2 - 2l m p)/((1+l^2)(l p + m)^2) - 2(l p + m)^2/((1+l^2)^2 (m+2l p)).

Now, recall that V = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x. So we need to compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of l,m,p. Let's compute M_x and N_x using the simplified forms.

We have M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C, N_x = (1+l^2)x_D^2 / D_D. Also, we have D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m. And x_C, x_D are roots of the quadratic. We can express x_C^2/D_C in terms of x_C and D_C. Since we have p x_C - m^2 = x_C D_C, we can also write x_C^2/D_C = (x_C (p x_C - m^2) - l m x_C^2)/(x_C D_C)? Not.

Maybe we can compute M_x+N_x directly using the relation we found earlier: M_x = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / D_C = (1+l^2)x_C^2 / ((1+l^2)x_C + l m). We can also write this as: M_x = x_C - (l m x_C)/((1+l^2)x_C + l m). Because x_C - (l m x_C)/(D_C) = x_C (1 - l m/D_C) = x_C ( (D_C - l m)/D_C ) = x_C ( (1+l^2)x_C / D_C ) = (1+l^2)x_C^2/D_C = M_x. So M_x = x_C - l m x_C / D_C. Similarly, N_x = x_D - l m x_D / D_D.

Thus, M_x+N_x = (x_C + x_D) - l m ( x_C/D_C + x_D/D_D ). And M_x N_x = (x_C - l m x_C/D_C)(x_D - l m x_D/D_D) = x_C x_D - l m ( x_C x_D/D_D + x_D x_C/D_C ) + l^2 m^2 x_C x_D/(D_C D_D). But maybe we can compute these using the quadratic.

We have x_C/D_C =? Since D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m, we can compute x_C/D_C = 1/( (1+l^2) + l m/x_C ). Not simple.

Alternatively, we can compute M_x+N_x using the formula we derived earlier: M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C, with K = p - l m. But we also have that (p x_C - m^2) = x_C D_C. So p x_C - m^2 = x_C D_C => p x_C = x_C D_C + m^2 => x_C (p - D_C) = m^2. But D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + l m, so p - D_C = p - (1+l^2)x_C - l m. Not helpful.

Given the time, I think we can trust that the identity holds because we have derived that M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D and that H_y is expressed nicely. Then we can check if that expression equals -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x. This is a heavy but straightforward algebraic verification. Perhaps we can compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x using the fact that we have expressions for the sum of the fractions. Let's attempt to compute M_x+N_x directly by adding the two expressions:

No, factor 2m^2/(1+l^2): = 2m^2/(1+l^2)[ (p - l m) - l m ] = 2m^2/(1+l^2)[ p - 2l m ]. That's not correct because the second term is 2l m^3/(1+l^2) = 2m^2 * (l m)/(1+l^2). So it's 2m^2/(1+l^2)[ (p - l m) - l m ] = 2m^2/(1+l^2)[ p - 2l m ]. Yes, so first part = 2m^2(p - 2l m)/(1+l^2). Then plus 4l m (p - l m)^2/(1+l^2)^2.

Now, D_C D_D = m(m+2l p). So:

x_C^2/D_C + x_D^2/D_D = N_sum / [ m(m+2l p) ] = [ 2m^2(p - 2l m)/(1+l^2) + 4l m (p - l m)^2/(1+l^2)^2 ] / [ m(m+2l p) ] = [ 2m(p - 2l m)/(1+l^2) + 4l (p - l m)^2/(1+l^2)^2 ] / (m+2l p).

So M_x+N_x = 2m(p - 2l m)/(m+2l p) + 4l (p - l m)^2/((1+l^2)(m+2l p)).

Now, V = -2m - 2l (M_x+N_x) - (2(1+l^2)/m) M_x N_x = -2 - 2*0.5*0.2667 - (2*1.25/1)*(-0.26667) = -2 -0.2667 - (2.5)*(-0.26667) = -2 -0.2667 +0.66667 = -1.6. So V = -1.6. Then V+H_y = -1.6+2.13333=0.53333. Then H_y (V+H_y)=2.13333*0.53333=1.13778? Actually, 2.13333*0.53333=1.13778. (M_x-N_x)^2 = (-0.4-0.6667)^2 = (-1.0667)^2=1.1378. So the identity holds. So we need to prove that generally.

Thus, the analytic proof is doable. We have:

- A = (0,0)
- P = (p,0) with p>0
- L: y = lx + m (m>0)
- Roots x_C, x_D of (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(lm - p)x + m^2 = 0. Then C = (x_C, y_C) with y_C = lx_C+m, D = (x_D, y_D) with y_D = lx_D+m.
- M = (M_x, M_y) with M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C, M_y = lM_x+m, where K = p - lm, D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm.
- N = (N_x, N_y) with N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/D_D, N_y = lN_x+m, where D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + lm.
- Then E = (2M_x, 0), F = (2N_x, 0).
- B = (b_x, b_y) = (-2lm/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2)).
- H = orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since P=(p,0) and M,N on L, we can compute H. The altitude from P is perpendicular to L, so its equation: y = - (1/l)(x - p) (since slope of L is l, perpendicular slope is -1/l). So H lies on this line: H_y = - (1/l)(H_x - p). Also, altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we can solve for H_y. We already derived that H_y = 2(lp+m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ]. Let's verify that derivation using the correct expressions.

We have H lies on altitude from P: y = - (1/l)(x - p). So x = p - l y. Now, we also have that H lies on altitude from M: (H - M) · (P - N) = 0. So (p - l y - M_x, y - M_y) · (p - N_x, -N_y) = 0. This is linear in y. So we can solve for y. This should give the expression above. We can compute that in terms of M_x, M_y, N_x, N_y. But we have already derived that H_y = 2(lp+m)^2/((1+l^2)(m+2lp)). That derivation used the fact that M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D, and that we had expressions for M_x and N_x. However, we can also derive it using the formula for orthocenter in terms of coordinates. Since we have explicit coordinates for P, M, N, we can compute H directly using the formula:

H = ( x_H, y_H ) = ( (x_P^2+y_P^2)(y_M - y_N) + (x_M^2+y_M^2)(y_N - y_P) + (x_N^2+y_N^2)(y_P - y_M) ) / (2 * (x_P(y_M - y_N) + x_M(y_N - y_P) + x_N(y_P - y_M) ) ), etc. That is heavy. But we have already a simpler derivation: Since the altitude from P is perpendicular to L, and the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, we can solve. That is what we did for the numeric test. So we can derive H_y in terms of l,m,p by substituting the expressions for M_x and N_x. That is doable and leads to the expression above. So we can accept that formula.

Now, we need to show that the horizontal line y = H_y is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. The circle through B, E, F. Since E and F are on x-axis, the circle's center O = ( (E_x+F_x)/2, (E_y+F_y)/2 )? Actually, the perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical line x = (E_x+F_x)/2. And the perpendicular bisector of BE is the line through the midpoint of BE and perpendicular to BE. So we can compute the center O = (x_O, y_O) and radius R = distance from O to E. The condition for the horizontal line y = H_y to be tangent is that the distance from O to the line equals the radius. That is: |y_O - H_y| = R. This is equivalent to (y_O - H_y)^2 = R^2. So we need to show that (y_O - H_y)^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + (y_O - 0)^2? Actually, R^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + (y_O - 0)^2. So we need:

(y_O - H_y)^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + y_O^2.

Thus, x_O^2 - 2x_O E_x + E_x^2 + y_O^2 - 2y_O H_y + H_y^2 = x_O^2 - 2x_O E_x + E_x^2 + y_O^2 => Cancel: -2y_O H_y + H_y^2 = 0 => H_y^2 = 2y_O H_y => H_y = 2y_O. So the condition is simply that y_O = H_y/2. Because the equation simplifies to H_y^2 = 2y_O H_y => if H_y ≠ 0, then H_y = 2y_O. So the tangency condition reduces to: the y-coordinate of the circumcenter of triangle BEF is exactly half of the y-coordinate of H. Since H is above x-axis, H_y>0, so y_O = H_y/2. So we need to prove that y_O = H_y/2.

Now, that is a much simpler condition. Let's verify with numeric test: H_y=2.13333, y_O=0.8, indeed y_O = H_y/2 = 1.06667? Wait, 2.13333/2=1.06667, but we got y_O=0.8. That doesn't match. In our numeric test, we had O = (0.2667,0.8). So y_O=0.8, H_y=2.13333, so H_y/2=1.06667, not 0.8. So something is wrong: The condition we derived from the tangency equation assumed that the circle passes through E and F on the x-axis, and that the horizontal line is tangent. Let's re-derive the condition.

We have circle: through points B(b_x,b_y), E(E_x,0), F(F_x,0). The center O = (x_O, y_O). The radius R = distance from O to E. For the horizontal line y = H_y to be tangent, we need that the distance from O to the line equals R. The distance from O to the line y=H_y is |y_O - H_y|. So condition: |y_O - H_y| = R. So (y_O - H_y)^2 = R^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + (y_O - 0)^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + y_O^2. Expand: y_O^2 - 2y_O H_y + H_y^2 = x_O^2 - 2x_O E_x + E_x^2 + y_O^2 => Cancel y_O^2: -2y_O H_y + H_y^2 = x_O^2 - 2x_O E_x + E_x^2. So H_y^2 - 2y_O H_y = x_O^2 - 2x_O E_x + E_x^2. This is not simply H_y = 2y_O. So my earlier simplification was incorrect because I assumed that x_O and E_x are related such that the right side simplifies to something like (x_O - E_x)^2, but I canceled terms incorrectly. Actually, the equation is: H_y^2 - 2y_O H_y = (x_O - E_x)^2 + E_x^2? Wait, x_O^2 - 2x_O E_x + E_x^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2. So the equation becomes: H_y^2 - 2y_O H_y = (x_O - E_x)^2. But that is not simply H_y = 2y_O. So the condition is:

H_y^2 - 2y_O H_y = (x_O - E_x)^2.

We also have that the circle passes through F, so (x_O - F_x)^2 + y_O^2 = R^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + y_O^2 => (x_O - F_x)^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2. This implies that either x_O is the midpoint of EF, or the distances are equal. In fact, the perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical line through the midpoint. Since the circle passes through E and F, the center must lie on the perpendicular bisector of EF. So x_O = (E_x + F_x)/2. That is true. So x_O is the midpoint. Then x_O - E_x = (F_x - E_x)/2. So (x_O - E_x)^2 = ((F_x - E_x)/2)^2. So the tangency condition becomes:

H_y^2 - 2y_O H_y = ((F_x - E_x)/2)^2.

Thus, we need to show that y_O = (H_y^2 - ((F_x - E_x)/2)^2) / (2H_y). Or equivalently, that y_O = (H_y/2) - ((F_x - E_x)^2)/(8H_y). But we also have that the circle passes through B. So we can compute y_O from the coordinates. So the condition is more complex.

But we can also use the fact that the circle passes through B, E, F. Since we have coordinates for E, F, B, we can find the circle equation. The y-coordinate of the center y_O can be expressed in terms of the points. There is a formula: For three points (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), the circumcenter's y-coordinate is given by something. But we already have a simpler relation from the tangency condition: The line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). Since AP is x-axis, the line is horizontal. So we need to show that the circle (BEF) is tangent to the horizontal line y=H_y. That is equivalent to the equation of the circle having exactly one solution when y=H_y. So we can substitute y=H_y into the circle equation and set the discriminant zero. But perhaps we can find a synthetic relation that simplifies.

Given the time, I think it's best to present a rigorous analytic proof using the coordinate system with A at origin and AP as x-axis. We can compute all necessary expressions in terms of l,m,p and the roots. We can show that the circumcenter of triangle BEF has y-coordinate equal to H_y/2? Wait, in our numeric test, H_y=2.13333, y_O=0.8, H_y/2=1.06667, not 0.8. So that's not.

But we can compute y_O directly from the coordinates. We have E=(2M_x,0), F=(2N_x,0). So x_O = (E_x+F_x)/2 = M_x+N_x. So x_O = M_x+N_x. Then the center O = (M_x+N_x, y_O). The radius squared R^2 = (x_O - E_x)^2 + y_O^2 = (M_x+N_x - 2M_x)^2 + y_O^2 = (N_x - M_x)^2 + y_O^2. So R^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2.

Now, the circle passes through B = (b_x,b_y). So (b_x - x_O)^2 + (b_y - y_O)^2 = R^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2. So (b_x - (M_x+N_x))^2 + (b_y - y_O)^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2. Expand: (b_x^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + (M_x+N_x)^2) + (b_y^2 - 2b_y y_O + y_O^2) = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2. Cancel y_O^2: b_x^2 + b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + (M_x+N_x)^2 - 2b_y y_O = (M_x - N_x)^2. So b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + (M_x+N_x)^2 - (M_x-N_x)^2 = 2b_y y_O. But (M_x+N_x)^2 - (M_x-N_x)^2 = 4M_x N_x. So we have:

b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x = 2b_y y_O.

Thus, y_O = [ b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x ] / (2b_y).

Now, we can compute this expression in terms of l,m,p. And then we need to show that the horizontal line y=H_y is tangent to the circle, i.e., that the distance from O to the line equals R. That is: |y_O - H_y| = R = sqrt( (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2 ). Squaring: (y_O - H_y)^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2 => y_O^2 - 2y_O H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2 => Cancel y_O^2: -2y_O H_y + H_y^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 => H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2y_O H_y => y_O = (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2) / (2H_y). So we need to prove that:

[ b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x ] / (2b_y) = (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2) / (2H_y).

Multiply both sides by 2: (b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x) / b_y = (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2) / H_y.

Thus, we need to show that:

H_y (b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x) = b_y (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2).

Now, we have expressions for all quantities in terms of l,m,p and the roots. This is a heavy but manageable algebraic identity. Since we have already derived that M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D, and that H_y = 2(lp+m)^2/((1+l^2)(m+2lp)). Also, b_x = -2lm/(1+l^2), b_y = 2m/(1+l^2). And M_x, N_x are given by M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C, N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/D_D, with K = p - lm. And D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm, D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + lm.

We can try to prove the identity by substituting these expressions and simplifying using the relations from the quadratic. This is doable but heavy. Given that the problem is from an olympiad, there must be a synthetic solution. However, as a proof generator, we can present an analytic solution with the steps and algebraic simplifications. I'll try to outline the verification in a clean way.

Let's denote:

- L: y = lx + m, with m > 0.
- Quadratic: (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(lm - p)x + m^2 = 0. Roots: x_C, x_D.
- Then:
  - x_C + x_D = 2(p - lm)/(1+l^2)
  - x_C x_D = m^2/(1+l^2)
  - Also, (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2(lm - p)x_C + m^2 = 0 => (1+l^2)x_C^2 = 2(p - lm)x_C - m^2 = 2K x_C - m^2.
  - Similarly for D.

- Define:
  - D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm
  - D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + lm
  - M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C
  - N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/D_D

- Then we have:
  - M_x N_x = (K x_C - m^2)(K x_D - m^2) / (D_C D_D)
  - M_x + N_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C + (K x_D - m^2)/D_D

- Also, b_x = -2lm/(1+l^2), b_y = 2m/(1+l^2). So b_x^2+b_y^2 = 4m^2/(1+l^2).

- H_y = 2(lp+m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ].

Now, we need to prove that:

H_y (b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x) = b_y (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2).

Let's compute the left side LHS = H_y * [4m^2/(1+l^2) - 2(-2lm/(1+l^2))(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x] = H_y * [4m^2/(1+l^2) + (4lm/(1+l^2))(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x].

Simplify: LHS = 4H_y * [ m^2/(1+l^2) + lm (M_x+N_x)/(1+l^2) + M_x N_x ] = 4H_y * [ (m^2 + lm (M_x+N_x))/(1+l^2) + M_x N_x ].

RHS = b_y (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2) = (2m/(1+l^2)) (H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2) = (2m/(1+l^2)) H_y^2 - (2m/(1+l^2)) (M_x - N_x)^2.

Now, note that (M_x - N_x)^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4M_x N_x. So we can write RHS = (2m/(1+l^2)) H_y^2 - (2m/(1+l^2)) [(M_x+N_x)^2 - 4M_x N_x] = (2m/(1+l^2)) H_y^2 - (2m/(1+l^2)) (M_x+N_x)^2 + (8m/(1+l^2)) M_x N_x.

Thus, the identity to prove becomes:

4H_y * [ (m^2 + lm (M_x+N_x))/(1+l^2) + M_x N_x ] = (2m/(1+l^2)) H_y^2 - (2m/(1+l^2)) (M_x+N_x)^2 + (8m/(1+l^2)) M_x N_x.

Multiply both sides by (1+l^2) to clear denominator:

4H_y (m^2 + lm (M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x) = 2m H_y^2 - 2m (M_x+N_x)^2 + 8m M_x N_x.

Divide both sides by 2:

2H_y (m^2 + lm (M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x) = m H_y^2 - m (M_x+N_x)^2 + 4m M_x N_x.

Now, bring all terms to one side:

m H_y^2 - m (M_x+N_x)^2 + 4m M_x N_x - 2H_y (m^2 + lm (M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2) M_x N_x) = 0.

Factor m? Not.

Now, we need to express M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of l,m,p. Let's compute these using the roots.

We have M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C, N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/D_D.

We can also compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x using the fact that D_C and D_D are related to x_C and x_D. Let's try to find expressions for the fractions.

First, note that K = p - lm. Also, from the circle equation, we have (1+l^2)x_C^2 = 2K x_C - m^2. So K x_C - m^2 = (1+l^2)x_C^2 - K x_C? Actually, K x_C - m^2 = (1+l^2)x_C^2 - K x_C. So M_x = ((1+l^2)x_C^2 - K x_C)/D_C = x_C((1+l^2)x_C - K)/D_C. Not simpler.

Alternatively, we can compute M_x in terms of x_C and D_C. Since D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm, we have (1+l^2)x_C = D_C - lm. So (1+l^2)x_C - K = D_C - lm - K. So M_x = x_C(D_C - lm - K)/D_C = x_C(1 - (lm+K)/D_C). But lm+K = lm + p - lm = p. So M_x = x_C(1 - p/D_C). So M_x = x_C - (p x_C)/D_C. That is interesting. Since x_C is on ω: x_C^2+y_C^2=2p x_C. But that doesn't directly give p x_C.

We can also compute M_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C. And note that p x_C - m^2 =? Not.

Let's compute M_x+N_x using the fact that we can combine fractions. But maybe we can find that M_x+N_x = (p x_C - m^2)/D_C + (p x_D - m^2)/D_D? Not.

Given the time, I think we can accept that the identity holds based on the algebraic simplifications we have done. However, we need to present a proof. We can present the analytic proof by showing that the circumcenter of BEF has y-coordinate equal to H_y/2? Wait, in our numeric test, H_y=2.13333, y_O=0.8, H_y/2=1.06667, not 0.8. So that's not. But we derived that the tangency condition is equivalent to: H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2y_O H_y. So we need to compute y_O from the circle equation. We have y_O = [ b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x ] / (2b_y). So we can compute that expression in terms of l,m,p. Then we can compute H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 and show that it equals 2y_O H_y. That is an identity that we can verify using the expressions.

Given the complexity, but the problem is from an olympiad, I think a synthetic solution is expected. I recall a known geometry problem: "Two circles intersect at A and B. Points C and D are on the circles such that C, M, N, D are collinear (M, N are centers). Let P be the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Prove that the line through the orthocenter of triangle PMN parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." This might be solved by using the fact that the circle (BEF) is the nine-point circle of triangle PCD? Let's test that: In our numeric test, triangle PCD: P(2,0), C(0.4,1.2), D(2,2). Compute its nine-point circle: midpoints of sides: PC midpoint = (1.2,0.6), PD midpoint = (2,1), CD midpoint = (1.2,1.6). The feet of altitudes: from P to CD? CD: from (0.4,1.2) to (2,2), slope = (0.8)/(1.6)=0.5, so line: y-1.2=0.5(x-0.4) => y=0.5x+1. The altitude from P: perpendicular slope = -2, through P(2,0): y=-2(x-2) => y=-2x+4. Intersection with CD: solve 0.5x+1 = -2x+4 => 2.5x=3 => x=1.2, y=0.5*1.2+1=1.6. So foot from P is (1.2,1.6) which is the midpoint of CD. That's interesting. So the nine-point circle passes through the midpoints and the foot. The center of nine-point circle is the midpoint of orthocenter and circumcenter. Compute circumcenter of PCD: we already found it's (1.2,? Actually, we can compute: The perpendicular bisector of PC: PC from (2,0) to (0.4,1.2), midpoint (1.2,0.6), slope of PC = (1.2-0)/(0.4-2)=1.2/(-1.6)=-0.75, so perpendicular slope = 4/3≈1.33333. Equation: y-0.6 = (4/3)(x-1.2). Perpendicular bisector of PD: P(2,0) to D(2,2), midpoint (2,1), line vertical? Actually, PD is vertical line x=2, so perpendicular bisector is horizontal line y=1. Intersection of y=1 with the other line: 1-0.6 = (4/3)(x-1.2) => 0.4 = (4/3)(x-1.2) => x-1.2 = 0.3 => x=1.5. So circumcenter O_circ = (1.5,1). Then orthocenter of PCD: Since CD is not horizontal, we need to find altitudes. Altitude from P: line through P perpendicular to CD, slope = -2, equation y=-2x+4. Altitude from C: line through C perpendicular to PD, PD is vertical, so altitude from C is horizontal line y=1.2. Intersection: y=1.2, then -2x+4=1.2 => 2x=2.8 => x=1.4. So orthocenter H_PCD = (1.4,1.2). Then nine-point center = midpoint of O_circ and H_PCD = ((1.5+1.4)/2, (1+1.2)/2) = (1.45,1.1). Radius = half of circumradius = distance from O_circ to P = sqrt((1.5-2)^2+(1-0)^2)= sqrt(0.25+1)= sqrt(1.25)=1.118, half=0.559. The circle (BEF) we computed had center (0.2667,0.8) radius 1.3333, not the nine-point circle. So no.

Maybe the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter from B to the point where the line through P perpendicular to MN meets the x-axis? Not.

Given the time, I think it's acceptable to present a computational proof with the simplifications we have made. Since the problem is to prove a geometric statement, an analytic proof is rigorous. I'll write it clearly.

I'll set up coordinates:

- Let M = (0,0) and N = (d,0) with d>0. Then the common chord AB is the perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting the centers? Actually, the line of centers is the x-axis. The circles intersect at A and B. By symmetry, the common chord AB is perpendicular to the x-axis, so it is a vertical line. Let A = (a, h) and B = (a, -h) with h>0. Then the radii satisfy: r = MA = sqrt(a^2+h^2), R = NA = sqrt((a-d)^2+h^2). The order C, M, N, D on the x-axis gives C = (-r, 0) and D = (d+R, 0).

- P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are on the x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through its midpoint: x = (C_x+D_x)/2 = ( -r + d+R )/2 = (d+R-r)/2. So P has x-coordinate p_x = (d+R-r)/2. The y-coordinate p_y is found from PA = PC. Using coordinates, we get p_y = (h^2 - r(d+R))/(2h). (One can derive that by solving the equation of the perpendicular bisector of AC or AD.)

- Then line AP has slope α = (p_y - h)/p_x = - (h^2 + r(d+R))/(h(d+R-r)). Also, the line AP passes through A(a,h). Its equation can be written as y = α x + b, where b = h - α a.

- Now, the second intersections of AP with the circles are:

  For Ω: (x)^2 + y^2 = r^2. Substituting y = α x + b gives (1+α^2)x^2 + 2α b x + b^2 = r^2. Since A satisfies this, we have a root x = a. The other root is x_E = -2α b/(1+α^2) - a. So E = (x_E, α x_E + b).

  For Γ: (x-d)^2 + y^2 = R^2. Substituting gives (1+α^2)x^2 + (2α b - 2d)x + (d^2 + b^2 - R^2) = 0. Since A satisfies, x = a is a root. The other root is x_F = 2(d - α b)/(1+α^2) - a. So F = (x_F, α x_F + b).

- B = (a, -h). So we have three points.

Now, we want to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. That is a condition that can be expressed in terms of coordinates. However, this approach is messy.

Given the complexity, I think the earlier coordinate system with A at origin and AP as x-axis is simpler. I'll adopt that.

So I'll write:

Place A at the origin, and let the line AP be the x-axis. Then P = (p,0) with p>0. Since A is an intersection of circles Ω and Γ, the other intersection B is the reflection of A across the line of centers. Let the line of centers be L: y = lx + m, with m>0. Then B = (b_x,b_y) = (-2lm/(1+l^2), 2m/(1+l^2)). The circles Ω and Γ are centered at M and N on L, and pass through A and B. The points C and D are the intersections of L with the circle ω (center P, radius |p|) that passes through A. Since A is at origin, ω: x^2+y^2 = 2px. Solving the intersection gives two points C and D with x-coordinates x_C, x_D satisfying (1+l^2)x^2 + 2(lm-p)x + m^2 = 0. Then we define M = circumcenter of A and C, so M lies on L and satisfies MA = MC. This gives M_x = (p x_C - m y_C)/ ((1+l^2)x_C + lm) = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C, where K = p - lm, D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm. Similarly, N_x = (K x_D - m^2)/D_D.

Then E = (2M_x, 0) and F = (2N_x, 0). Now, compute H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since P is on x-axis and M,N on L, we can find H. The altitude from P is perpendicular to L, so its equation: y = - (1/l)(x-p). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. Solving gives H_y = 2(lp+m)^2 / [(1+l^2)(m+2lp)]. (One can verify by solving the linear system.) Then the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal: y = H_y.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since E and F lie on the x-axis, the circle's center O = (M_x+N_x, y_O) because the perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical through its midpoint x = (E_x+F_x)/2 = M_x+N_x. The y-coordinate y_O can be found from the condition that O is equidistant from B and E. This gives:

y_O = [ b_x^2+b_y^2 - 2b_x(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x ] / (2b_y).

Substituting b_x = -2lm/(1+l^2), b_y = 2m/(1+l^2), we have:

y_O = [ 4m^2/(1+l^2) + (4lm/(1+l^2))(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x ] / (2 * 2m/(1+l^2)) = [ 4m^2/(1+l^2) + (4lm/(1+l^2))(M_x+N_x) + 4M_x N_x ] * (1+l^2)/(4m) = [ m^2 + lm(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2)M_x N_x ] / m.

Thus, y_O = m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2)M_x N_x / m.

Now, we need to show that the horizontal line y = H_y is tangent to this circle. The distance from O to the line is |y_O - H_y|. The radius R = distance from O to E: R^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2. The tangency condition is:

(y_O - H_y)^2 = (M_x - N_x)^2 + y_O^2 => H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2y_O H_y.

Thus, we need to prove that:

H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2y_O H_y.

Substituting y_O from above, we need:

H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2H_y [ m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2)M_x N_x / m ].

Now, we can compute H_y^2 and (M_x - N_x)^2 in terms of l,m,p and the roots. We have:

M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D (as we can derive by algebraic manipulation). Indeed, using the expressions for M_x and N_x, we can compute:

M_x - N_x = (K x_C - m^2)/D_C - (K x_D - m^2)/D_D = ( (K x_C - m^2)D_D - (K x_D - m^2)D_C )/(D_C D_D). The numerator simplifies to (x_C - x_D)( (1+l^2)x_C x_D + l m (x_C+x_D) + m^2? Actually, we can use the identities from the quadratic. We have already derived that M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D in the numeric test, and we can prove it generally by substituting the expressions and using the relations. Let's do that.

We have D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm, D_D = (1+l^2)x_D + lm.
Also, K x_C - m^2 =? From the quadratic: (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2(lm-p)x_C + m^2 = 0 => (1+l^2)x_C^2 = 2(p-lm)x_C - m^2 = 2K x_C - m^2. So K x_C - m^2 = (1+l^2)x_C^2 - K x_C. So M_x = ((1+l^2)x_C^2 - K x_C)/D_C = x_C((1+l^2)x_C - K)/D_C. Similarly, N_x = x_D((1+l^2)x_D - K)/D_D.

Now, compute M_x - N_x = x_C((1+l^2)x_C - K)/D_C - x_D((1+l^2)x_D - K)/D_D. This is not obviously x_C - x_D. However, we can use the fact that D_C = (1+l^2)x_C + lm, so ((1+l^2)x_C - K) = D_C - lm - K. And lm+K = p. So ((1+l^2)x_C - K) = D_C - p. So M_x = x_C(D_C - p)/D_C = x_C - (p x_C)/D_C. So M_x = x_C - p x_C/D_C. Similarly, N_x = x_D - p x_D/D_D. Then M_x - N_x = (x_C - x_D) - p( x_C/D_C - x_D/D_D). Now, we need to show that p( x_C/D_C - x_D/D_D) = 0. That would give M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D. Is it true that x_C/D_C = x_D/D_D? Let's test with numeric: x_C=0.4, D_C=1, so x_C/D_C=0.4. x_D=2, D_D=3, so x_D/D_D=2/3≈0.6667. Not equal. So p(0.4-0.6667) is not zero. So M_x - N_x is not equal to x_C - x_D. But in our numeric test, we got M_x = -0.4, N_x = 0.6667, so M_x - N_x = -1.0667, while x_C - x_D = -1.6. So they are not equal. So my earlier claim that M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D is false. In fact, we had M_x - N_x = -1.0667, and x_C - x_D = -1.6. So they are different. But we also have M_x - N_x = -1.0667, and (M_x - N_x)^2 = 1.1378. (x_C - x_D)^2 = 2.56. So not equal. So the simplification that M_x - N_x = x_C - x_D is not generally true. However, in the numeric test, we have M_x - N_x =? We had M_x = -0.4, N_x = 0.6667, difference = -1.0667. So that is not x_C - x_D = -1.6. So we need to compute (M_x - N_x)^2 correctly in terms of l,m,p. And also H_y^2. Then we can verify the tangency condition.

Given the time, I think we can present an analytic proof that uses the explicit expressions for M_x and N_x in terms of x_C, x_D and then show that the equation H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2y_O H_y reduces to an identity that holds because of the quadratic relations. This is heavy but doable. However, as a proof generator, I can outline the steps and simplify using symmetric sums. I'll try to compute these expressions in a systematic way.

Let's define:

- Let t = x_C and u = x_D. Then t+u = 2K/(1+l^2) (since K = p-lm, but careful: from quadratic, t+u = 2(p-lm)/(1+l^2) = 2K/(1+l^2)). And t u = m^2/(1+l^2).

- D_C = (1+l^2)t + lm, D_D = (1+l^2)u + lm.
- M_x = (K t - m^2)/D_C, N_x = (K u - m^2)/D_D.

Now, we can compute M_x+N_x and M_x N_x in terms of t,u, and l,m. Let's compute:

M_x+N_x = (K t - m^2)/D_C + (K u - m^2)/D_D = (K t - m^2)D_D + (K u - m^2)D_C / (D_C D_D). And D_C D_D = ((1+l^2)t + lm)((1+l^2)u + lm) = (1+l^2)^2 tu + lm(1+l^2)(t+u) + l^2 m^2 = (1+l^2)^2 * (m^2/(1+l^2)) + lm(1+l^2)* (2K/(1+l^2)) + l^2 m^2 = (1+l^2)m^2 + 2lmK + l^2 m^2 = m^2(1+l^2) + 2lmK + l^2 m^2 = m^2 + l^2 m^2 + 2lmK + l^2 m^2 = m^2 + 2l^2 m^2 + 2lmK? Wait, I think I made a mistake: (1+l^2)^2 tu = (1+l^2)^2 * (m^2/(1+l^2)) = (1+l^2)m^2. Then lm(1+l^2)(t+u) = lm(1+l^2)*(2K/(1+l^2)) = 2lmK. Then l^2 m^2. So sum = (1+l^2)m^2 + 2lmK + l^2 m^2 = m^2(1+l^2) + l^2 m^2 + 2lmK = m^2 + l^2 m^2 + l^2 m^2 + 2lmK = m^2 + 2l^2 m^2 + 2lmK. That is not m(m+2lp) as earlier? Let's compute with numeric: l=0.5, m=1, p=2 => K=2-0.5=1.5, then (1+l^2)=1.25, tu=1/1.25=0.8, t+u=2*1.5/1.25=3/1.25=2.4. Then D_C D_D = (1.25*0.4+0.5)*(1.25*2+0.5)= (0.5+0.5)*(2.5+0.5)=1*3=3. And m(m+2lp)=1*(1+2*0.5*2)=1*(1+2)=3. So the product equals m(m+2lp). In our derivation, we got m^2+2l^2 m^2+2lmK. Plug numbers: m^2=1, 2l^2 m^2=2*0.25*1=0.5, 2lmK=2*0.5*1*1.5=1.5, sum=3. So indeed m^2+2l^2 m^2+2lmK = m(m+2lp) because m+2lp = 1+2=3, product=3. So identity: m^2+2l^2 m^2+2lmK = m(m+2lp). So D_C D_D = m(m+2lp). Good.

Now, we need to compute numerator of M_x+N_x: N_sum = (K t - m^2)D_D + (K u - m^2)D_C. Expand:

N_sum = (K t - m^2)((1+l^2)u + lm) + (K u - m^2)((1+l^2)t + lm)
= (1+l^2)[(K t - m^2)u + (K u - m^2)t] + lm[(K t - m^2) + (K u - m^2)]
= (1+l^2)[K t u - m^2 u + K t u - m^2 t] + lm[K(t+u) - 2m^2]
= (1+l^2)[2K t u - m^2(t+u)] + lm[K(t+u) - 2m^2].

Now, substitute t u = m^2/(1+l^2), t+u = 2K/(1+l^2). Then:

2K t u = 2K * m^2/(1+l^2) = 2K m^2/(1+l^2).
m^2(t+u) = m^2 * 2K/(1+l^2) = 2K m^2/(1+l^2).
So the first term: (1+l^2)[2K m^2/(1+l^2) - 2K m^2/(1+l^2)] = 0. So the first part cancels. Then:

N_sum = lm[ K(t+u) - 2m^2 ] = lm[ K * (2K/(1+l^2)) - 2m^2 ] = lm[ 2K^2/(1+l^2) - 2m^2 ] = 2lm[ K^2/(1+l^2) - m^2 ].

Thus, M_x+N_x = N_sum / (D_C D_D) = 2lm[ K^2/(1+l^2) - m^2 ] / [ m(m+2lp) ] = 2l[ K^2/(1+l^2) - m^2 ] / (m+2lp).

Now, K = p - lm. So K^2 = (p - lm)^2. So M_x+N_x = 2l[ (p-lm)^2/(1+l^2) - m^2 ] / (m+2lp). Simplify: (p-lm)^2/(1+l^2) - m^2 = [ (p-lm)^2 - m^2(1+l^2) ]/(1+l^2) = [ p^2 - 2lmp + l^2 m^2 - m^2 - l^2 m^2 ]/(1+l^2) = [ p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp ]/(1+l^2). So M_x+N_x = 2l * (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp)/( (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ).

Thus, M_x+N_x = 2l * (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ].

Now, compute M_x N_x = (K t - m^2)(K u - m^2) / (D_C D_D). Compute numerator: (K t - m^2)(K u - m^2) = K^2 t u - K m^2 (t+u) + m^4 = K^2 * m^2/(1+l^2) - K m^2 * (2K/(1+l^2)) + m^4 = (K^2 m^2 - 2K^2 m^2)/(1+l^2) + m^4 = -K^2 m^2/(1+l^2) + m^4 = m^4 - K^2 m^2/(1+l^2). So M_x N_x = [ m^4 - K^2 m^2/(1+l^2) ] / [ m(m+2lp) ] = m^2 [ m^2 - K^2/(1+l^2) ] / [ m(m+2lp) ] = m [ m^2 - K^2/(1+l^2) ] / (m+2lp). But m^2 - K^2/(1+l^2) = [ m^2(1+l^2) - K^2 ]/(1+l^2) = [ m^2 + l^2 m^2 - (p-lm)^2 ]/(1+l^2) = [ m^2 + l^2 m^2 - p^2 + 2lmp - l^2 m^2 ]/(1+l^2) = [ m^2 - p^2 + 2lmp ]/(1+l^2) = [ - (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) ]/(1+l^2) = - (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp)/(1+l^2). So M_x N_x = m * [ - (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp)/(1+l^2) ] / (m+2lp) = - m (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ].

Thus, M_x N_x = - m (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ].

Now, note that M_x+N_x = 2l * (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ] = 2l * S, where S = (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp)/[ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ].

And M_x N_x = - m S.

Now, compute y_O = m + l(M_x+N_x) + (1+l^2)M_x N_x / m = m + l*(2l S) + (1+l^2)*(- m S)/m = m + 2l^2 S - (1+l^2) S = m + (2l^2 - 1 - l^2) S = m + (l^2 - 1) S.

Thus, y_O = m + (l^2 - 1) S.

Now, compute H_y = 2(lp+m)^2 / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ]. Let's also compute S = (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp)/[ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ]. Note that (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) = (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp). And (lp+m)^2 = l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2. So H_y^2 = 4(lp+m)^4 / [ (1+l^2)^2 (m+2lp)^2 ].

Now, we need to prove that H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = 2y_O H_y. But we also have M_x - N_x. We can compute M_x - N_x using the expressions we have. Since we have M_x+N_x and M_x N_x, we can compute (M_x - N_x)^2 = (M_x+N_x)^2 - 4M_x N_x = (2l S)^2 - 4(-m S) = 4l^2 S^2 + 4m S = 4S(l^2 S + m). So (M_x - N_x)^2 = 4S(l^2 S + m).

Now, we also have H_y^2. Let's compute H_y^2 - (M_x - N_x)^2 = H_y^2 - 4S(l^2 S + m). And 2y_O H_y = 2H_y (m + (l^2-1)S) = 2H_y m + 2H_y (l^2-1)S.

So we need to show:

H_y^2 - 4S(l^2 S + m) = 2H_y m + 2H_y (l^2-1)S.

Rearrange: H_y^2 - 2H_y m - 2H_y (l^2-1)S - 4S(l^2 S + m) = 0.

Now, substitute H_y and S. Let's compute H_y and S in terms of l,m,p.

Define:

- A = p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp
- B = (lp+m)^2 = l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2
- Then S = A / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ]
- H_y = 2B / [ (1+l^2)(m+2lp) ]

Thus, H_y = 2B / D, S = A / D, where D = (1+l^2)(m+2lp). So H_y = 2B/D, S = A/D.

Now, plug into the equation:

(4B^2/D^2) - 2*(2B/D)*m - 2*(2B/D)*(l^2-1)*(A/D) - 4*(A/D)*(l^2*(A/D) + m) = 0.

Multiply both sides by D^2 to clear denominators:

4B^2 - 4B m D - 4B (l^2-1) A - 4A (l^2 A + m D) = 0.

Divide by 4:

B^2 - B m D - B (l^2-1) A - A (l^2 A + m D) = 0.

Expand: B^2 - B m D - B (l^2-1) A - l^2 A^2 - m A D = 0.

Group terms: B^2 - l^2 A^2 - (B m D + m A D) - B (l^2-1) A = 0 => B^2 - l^2 A^2 - m D (B + A) - B (l^2-1) A = 0.

Now, note that B = (lp+m)^2, A = p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp. Compute B^2 - l^2 A^2. Since B and A are related, maybe we can factor.

Compute B^2 - l^2 A^2 = (B - lA)(B + lA). Compute B - lA: l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2 - l(p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) = l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2 - l p^2 + l m^2 + 2l^2 m p = l^2 p^2 - l p^2 + 2lmp + 2l^2 m p + m^2 + l m^2 = l p^2 (l-1) + 2lmp(1+l) + m^2(1+l). Not obviously zero. Compute B + lA: l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2 + l(p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) = l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2 + l p^2 - l m^2 - 2l^2 m p = l^2 p^2 + l p^2 + 2lmp - 2l^2 m p + m^2 - l m^2 = l p^2 (l+1) + 2lmp(1 - l) + m^2(1-l). Not simple.

Given the time, I think we can verify that the identity holds by using the fact that A and B are related by: (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp) =? Actually, from the quadratic, we have (1+l^2)x_C^2 + 2(lm-p)x_C + m^2 = 0. But that doesn't directly give a relation between A and B.

Maybe we can compute B^2 - l^2 A^2 directly using the expressions. Let's compute:

A = p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp.
B = l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2.

Compute B^2: (l^2 p^2 + 2lmp + m^2)^2 = l^4 p^4 + 4l^3 m p^3 + 2l^2 m^2 p^2? Actually, expand: (a+b+c)^2 = a^2+b^2+c^2+2ab+2ac+2bc. So:

a = l^2 p^2, b = 2lmp, c = m^2.
a^2 = l^4 p^4.
b^2 = 4l^2 m^2 p^2.
c^2 = m^4.
2ab = 2 * l^2 p^2 * 2lmp = 4l^3 m p^3.
2ac = 2 * l^2 p^2 * m^2 = 2l^2 m^2 p^2.
2bc = 2 * 2lmp * m^2 = 4l m^3 p.
So B^2 = l^4 p^4 + 4l^3 m p^3 + (4l^2 m^2 p^2 + 2l^2 m^2 p^2) + 4l m^3 p + m^4 = l^4 p^4 + 4l^3 m p^3 + 6l^2 m^2 p^2 + 4l m^3 p + m^4.

Now, compute l^2 A^2: A^2 = (p^2 - m^2 - 2lmp)^2. Expand: (p^2)^2 + (m^2)^2 + (2lmp)^2 + 2(p^2)(-m^2) + 2(p^2)(-2lmp) + 2(-m^2)(-2lmp) = p^4 + m^4 + 4l^2 m^2 p^2 - 2p^2 m^2 - 4lmp^3 + 4l m^3 p. So A^2 = p^4 - 2p^2 m^2 + m^4 + 4l^2 m^2 p^2 - 4lmp^3 + 4l m^3 p. Then l^2 A^2 = l^2 p^4 - 2l^2 p^2 m^2 + l^2 m^4 + 4l^4 m^2 p^2 - 4l^3 m p^3 + 4l^3 m^3 p.

Now, B^2 - l^2 A^2 = l^4 p^4 + 4l^3 m p^3 + 6l^2 m^2 p^2 + 4l m^3 p + m^4 - (l^2 p^4 - 2l^2 p^2 m^2 + l^2 m^4 + 4l^4 m^2 p^2 - 4l^3 m p^3 + 4l^3 m^3 p) = l^4 p^4 - l^2 p^4 + 4l^3 m p^3 + 4l^3 m p^3? Actually, careful: 4l^3 m p^3 - (-4l^3 m p^3) = 4l^3 m p^3 + 4l^3 m p^3 = 8l^3 m p^3. Next, 6l^2 m^2 p^2 - ( -2l^2 p^2 m^2 + 4l^4 m^2 p^2 ) = 6l^2 m^2 p^2 + 2l^2 m^2 p^2 - 4l^4 m^2 p^2 = 8l^2 m^2 p^2 - 4l^4 m^2 p^2. Next, 4l m^3 p - 4l^3 m^3 p = 4l m^3 p (1 - l^2). Then m^4 - l^2 m^4 = m^4(1 - l^2). So B^2 - l^2 A^2 = p^4(l^4 - l^2) + 8l^3 m p^3 + (8l^2 m^2 p^2 - 4l^4 m^2 p^2) + 4l m^3 p (1-l^2) + m^4(1-l^2). Factor (1-l^2)?
